   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Switzerland</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div>

    <div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" lang="en" dir="ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Country situated at the confluence of Western, Central, and Southern Europe
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       This article is about the sovereign state. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Switzerland_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Switzerland (disambiguation)">Switzerland (disambiguation)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Confederation_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Swiss Confederation (disambiguation)">Swiss Confederation (disambiguation)</a>.
      </div>

      <p><b>Switzerland</b>, officially the <b>Swiss Confederation</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked country</a> situated at the confluence of <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Southern Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[note 4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[14]</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Federal_republic" title="Federal republic">federal republic</a> composed of <a href="/wiki/Cantons_of_Switzerland" title="Cantons of Switzerland">26&nbsp;cantons</a>, with federal authorities based in <a href="/wiki/Bern" title="Bern">Bern</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fedcit_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fedcit-1">[note 1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-deFacto_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deFacto-3">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bundesstadt_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bundesstadt-2">[1]</a></sup> Switzerland is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> to the south, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> to the north, and <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a> to the east. It is geographically divided among the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Plateau" title="Swiss Plateau">Swiss Plateau</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Alps" title="Swiss Alps">Alps</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Jura_Mountains" title="Jura Mountains">Jura</a>, spanning a total area of 41,285&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (15,940&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi), and land area of 39,997&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (15,443&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi). Although the Alps occupy the greater part of the territory, the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_people" title="Swiss people">Swiss population</a> of approximately 8.5 million is concentrated mostly on the plateau, where the largest cities and economic centres are located, among them <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a>. These cities are home to several offices of international organisations such as the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_headquarters" title="FIFA headquarters">headquarters</a> of <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a>'s <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Office_at_Geneva" title="United Nations Office at Geneva">second-largest Office</a>, and the main building of the <a href="/wiki/Bank_for_International_Settlements" title="Bank for International Settlements">Bank for International Settlements</a>. The main international airports of Switzerland are also located in these cities. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_the_Old_Swiss_Confederacy" title="Growth of the Old Swiss Confederacy">establishment</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Swiss_Confederacy" title="Old Swiss Confederacy">Old Swiss Confederacy</a> dates to the <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">late medieval period</a>, resulting from a series of military successes against <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Austria" title="Duchy of Austria">Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Burgundy" title="Duchy of Burgundy">Burgundy</a>. Swiss independence from the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> was formally recognized in the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a> in 1648. The Federal Charter of 1291 is considered the founding document of Switzerland which is celebrated on <a href="/wiki/Swiss_National_Day" title="Swiss National Day">Swiss National Day</a>. Since the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Swiss Reformation">Reformation</a> of the 16th century, Switzerland has maintained a strong policy of <a href="/wiki/Armed_neutrality" class="mw-redirect" title="Armed neutrality">armed neutrality</a>; it has not fought an international war since 1815 and did not join the United Nations until 2002. Nevertheless, it pursues an active foreign policy and is frequently involved in peace-building processes around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[15]</a></sup> Switzerland is the birthplace of the <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">Red Cross</a>, one of the world's oldest and best known humanitarian organisations, and is home to <a href="/wiki/International_organisations_in_Europe" title="International organisations in Europe">numerous international organisations</a>, including the United Nations Office at Geneva, which is its second-largest in the world. It is a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">European Free Trade Association</a>, but notably not part of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">Eurozone</a>. However, it participates in the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Area" title="Schengen Area">Schengen Area</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Single_Market" title="European Single Market">European Single Market</a> through bilateral treaties. </p>
      <p>Switzerland occupies the crossroads of <a href="/wiki/Germanic-speaking_Europe" title="Germanic-speaking Europe">Germanic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance</a> Europe, as reflected in its four main linguistic and cultural regions: German, French, Italian and <a href="/wiki/Romansh_language" title="Romansh language">Romansh</a>. Although the majority of the population are German-speaking, <a href="/wiki/Swiss_people" title="Swiss people">Swiss</a> national identity is rooted in a common historical background, shared values such as <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[16]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alpine</a> symbolism.<sup id="cite_ref-j1_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-j1-21">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[18]</a></sup> Due to its linguistic diversity, Switzerland is known by a variety of native names: <i>Schweiz</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈʃvaɪts]</a></span> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>);<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[note 5]</a></sup> <i>Suisse</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[sɥis(ə)]</a></span> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>); <i>Svizzera</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">[ˈzvittsera]</a></span> (<a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>); and <i>Svizra</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Romansh" title="Help:IPA/Romansh">[ˈʒviːtsrɐ, ˈʒviːtsʁɐ]</a></span> (<a href="/wiki/Romansh_language" title="Romansh language">Romansh</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[note 6]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_Swiss_franc" title="Coins of the Swiss franc">coins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamps_and_postal_history_of_Switzerland" title="Postage stamps and postal history of Switzerland">stamps</a>, the Latin name, <i>Confoederatio Helvetica</i> – frequently shortened to "<a href="/wiki/Helvetia" title="Helvetia">Helvetia</a>" – is used instead of the four national languages. </p>
      <p>A <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed country</a>, it has the highest nominal <a href="/wiki/Wealth" title="Wealth">wealth</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_wealth_per_adult" title="List of countries by wealth per adult">per adult</a><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[19]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">eighth-highest</a> <a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per capita</a> <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a>, and has been considered a <a href="/wiki/Tax_haven" title="Tax haven">tax haven</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[21]</a></sup> It ranks highly on some international metrics, including <a href="/wiki/Global_Competitiveness_Report" title="Global Competitiveness Report">economic competitiveness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">human development</a>. Its cities such as Zürich, Geneva, and Basel rank among the highest in the world in terms of quality of life,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[23]</a></sup> albeit with some of the highest <a href="/wiki/Costs_of_living" class="mw-redirect" title="Costs of living">costs of living</a> in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[24]</a></sup> In 2020, <a href="/wiki/International_Institute_for_Management_Development" title="International Institute for Management Development">IMD</a> placed Switzerland first in attracting skilled workers.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[25]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a> ranks it the 5th most competitive country globally.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[26]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Switzerland" title="Name of Switzerland">Name of Switzerland</a>
      </div> 
      <p><span class="anchor" id="Etymology"></span> The English name <i>Switzerland</i> is a compound containing <i>Switzer</i>, an obsolete term for the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_people" title="Swiss people">Swiss</a>, which was in use during the 16th to 19th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[27]</a></sup> The English adjective <i>Swiss</i> is a loan from French <i><i lang="fr" title="French-language text">Suisse</i></i>, also in use since the 16th century. The name <i>Switzer</i> is from the <a href="/wiki/Alemannic_German" title="Alemannic German">Alemannic</a> <i><i lang="gsw" title="Alemannic German-language text">Schwiizer</i></i>, in origin an inhabitant of <i><a href="/wiki/Schwyz" title="Schwyz">Schwyz</a></i> and its <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Schwyz" title="Canton of Schwyz">associated territory</a>, one of the Waldstätte cantons which formed the nucleus of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Swiss_Confederacy" title="Old Swiss Confederacy">Old Swiss Confederacy</a>. The Swiss began to adopt the name for themselves after the <a href="/wiki/Swabian_War" title="Swabian War">Swabian War</a> of 1499, used alongside the term for "Confederates", <i><a href="/wiki/Eidgenosse" class="mw-redirect" title="Eidgenosse">Eidgenossen</a></i> (literally: <i>comrades by oath</i>), used since the 14th century. <span class="anchor" id="CH"></span>The <a href="/wiki/Data_codes_for_Switzerland#Country" title="Data codes for Switzerland">data code for Switzerland</a>, CH, is derived from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>Confoederatio Helvetica</i> (English: <span lang="en">Helvetic Confederation</span>). </p>
      <p>The toponym <i>Schwyz</i> itself was first attested in 972, as <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i><i lang="goh" title="Old High German (ca. 750-1050)-language text">Suittes</i></i>, ultimately perhaps related to <i><i lang="goh" title="Old High German (ca. 750-1050)-language text">swedan</i></i> ‘to burn’ (cf. <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>svíða</i> ‘to singe, burn’), referring to the area of forest that was burned and cleared to build.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[28]</a></sup> The name was extended to the area dominated by the canton, and after the Swabian War of 1499 gradually came to be used for the entire Confederation.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[30]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Swiss_German" title="Swiss German">Swiss German</a> name of the country, <i><i lang="gsw" title="Alemannic German-language text">Schwiiz</i></i>, is homophonous to that of the canton and the settlement, but distinguished by the use of the definite article (<i><i lang="gsw" title="Alemannic German-language text">d'Schwiiz</i></i> for the Confederation,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[31]</a></sup> but simply <i><i lang="gsw" title="Alemannic German-language text">Schwyz</i></i> for the canton and the town).<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[32]</a></sup> The long [iː] of Swiss German is historically and still often today spelled ⟨y⟩ rather than ⟨ii⟩, preserving the original identity of the two names even in writing. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> name <i>Confoederatio Helvetica</i> was <a href="/wiki/Neologism" title="Neologism">neologized</a> and introduced gradually after the <a href="/wiki/Switzerland_as_a_federal_state" title="Switzerland as a federal state">formation of the federal state</a> in 1848, harking back to the Napoleonic <a href="/wiki/Helvetic_Republic" title="Helvetic Republic">Helvetic Republic</a>, appearing on coins from 1879, inscribed on the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Palace_of_Switzerland" title="Federal Palace of Switzerland">Federal Palace</a> in 1902 and after 1948 used in the official seal<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[33]</a></sup> (e.g., the <a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">ISO banking code</a> "CHF" for the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_franc" title="Swiss franc">Swiss franc</a>, and the country top-level domain ".ch", are both taken from the state's Latin name). <i>Helvetica</i> is derived from the <i><a href="/wiki/Helvetii" title="Helvetii">Helvetii</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Gaulish_tribe" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaulish tribe">Gaulish tribe</a> living on the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_plateau" class="mw-redirect" title="Swiss plateau">Swiss plateau</a> before the <a href="/wiki/Switzerland_in_the_Roman_era" title="Switzerland in the Roman era">Roman era</a>. </p>
      <p><i><a href="/wiki/Helvetia" title="Helvetia">Helvetia</a></i> appears as a <a href="/wiki/National_personification" title="National personification">national personification</a> of the Swiss confederacy in the 17th century with a 1672 play by Johann Caspar Weissenbach.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[34]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Switzerland" title="History of Switzerland">History of Switzerland</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Switzerland has existed as a state in its present form since the adoption of the Swiss Federal Constitution in 1848. The precursors of Switzerland established a protective alliance at the end of the 13th century (1291), forming a loose confederation of states which persisted for centuries. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Early_history_of_Switzerland" title="Early history of Switzerland">Early history of Switzerland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland_in_the_Roman_era" title="Switzerland in the Roman era">Switzerland in the Roman era</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The oldest traces of hominid existence in Switzerland date back about 150,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early-41">[35]</a></sup> The oldest known farming settlements in Switzerland, which were found at <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A4chlingen" title="Gächlingen">Gächlingen</a>, have been dated to around 5300 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early-41">[35]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Theater_Kaiseraugst.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Theater_Kaiseraugst.jpg/220px-Theater_Kaiseraugst.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Theater_Kaiseraugst.jpg/330px-Theater_Kaiseraugst.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Theater_Kaiseraugst.jpg/440px-Theater_Kaiseraugst.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3856" data-file-height="2068"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Theater_Kaiseraugst.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Founded in 44 BC by <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Munatius_Plancus" title="Lucius Munatius Plancus">Lucius Munatius Plancus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Augusta_Raurica" title="Augusta Raurica">Augusta Raurica</a> (near Basel) was the first Roman settlement on the Rhine and is now among the most important archaeological sites in Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[36]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The earliest known cultural tribes of the area were members of the <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt</a> and <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_culture" title="La Tène culture">La Tène cultures</a>, named after the archaeological site of La Tène on the north side of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Neuch%C3%A2tel" title="Lake Neuchâtel">Lake Neuchâtel</a>. La Tène culture developed and flourished during the late <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> from around 450 BC,<sup id="cite_ref-Early_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early-41">[35]</a></sup> possibly under some influence from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_civilization" title="Etruscan civilization">Etruscan</a> civilisations. One of the most important tribal groups in the Swiss region was the <a href="/wiki/Helvetii" title="Helvetii">Helvetii</a>. Steadily harassed by the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic tribes</a>, in 58 BC the Helvetii decided to abandon the Swiss plateau and migrate to western <a href="/wiki/Gallia" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallia">Gallia</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>'s armies pursued and defeated them at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bibracte" title="Battle of Bibracte">Battle of Bibracte</a>, in today's eastern France, forcing the tribe to move back to its original homeland.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early-41">[35]</a></sup> In 15 BC, <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a>, who would one day become the second Roman emperor, and his brother <a href="/wiki/Nero_Claudius_Drusus" title="Nero Claudius Drusus">Drusus</a>, conquered the Alps, integrating them into the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. The area occupied by the Helvetii—the namesakes of the later <i>Confoederatio Helvetica</i>—first became part of Rome's <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Belgica" title="Gallia Belgica">Gallia Belgica</a> province and then of its <a href="/wiki/Germania_Superior" title="Germania Superior">Germania Superior</a> province, while the eastern portion of modern Switzerland was integrated into the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Raetia" title="Raetia">Raetia</a>. Sometime around the start of the Common Era, the Romans maintained a large legionary camp called <a href="/wiki/Vindonissa" title="Vindonissa">Vindonissa</a>, now a ruin at the confluence of the <a href="/wiki/Aare" title="Aare">Aare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reuss_(river)" title="Reuss (river)">Reuss</a> rivers, near the town of <a href="/wiki/Windisch" title="Windisch">Windisch</a>, an outskirt of <a href="/wiki/Brugg" title="Brugg">Brugg</a>. </p>
      <p>The first and second century AD was an age of prosperity for the population living on the Swiss plateau. Several towns, like <a href="/wiki/Aventicum" title="Aventicum">Aventicum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iulia_Equestris" class="mw-redirect" title="Iulia Equestris">Iulia Equestris</a> and Augusta Raurica, reached a remarkable size, while hundreds of agricultural estates (<a href="/wiki/Villae_rusticae" class="mw-redirect" title="Villae rusticae">Villae rusticae</a>) were founded in the countryside. </p>
      <p>Around 260 AD, the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Agri_Decumates" title="Agri Decumates">Agri Decumates</a> territory north of the Rhine transformed today's Switzerland into a frontier land of the Empire. Repeated raids by the <a href="/wiki/Alamanni" class="mw-redirect" title="Alamanni">Alamanni</a> tribes provoked the ruin of the Roman towns and economy, forcing the population to find shelter near Roman fortresses, like the <a href="/wiki/Castrum_Rauracense" class="mw-redirect" title="Castrum Rauracense">Castrum Rauracense</a> near Augusta Raurica. The Empire built another line of defence at the north border (the so-called Donau-Iller-Rhine-Limes), but at the end of the fourth century the increased Germanic pressure forced the Romans to abandon the linear defence concept, and the Swiss plateau was finally open to the settlement of Germanic tribes. </p>
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>, from the end of the 4th century, the western extent of modern-day Switzerland was part of the territory of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Burgundy" title="Kingdom of Burgundy">Kings of the Burgundians</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Alemanni" title="Alemanni">Alemanni</a> settled the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_plateau" class="mw-redirect" title="Swiss plateau">Swiss plateau</a> in the 5th century and the <a href="/wiki/Valleys_of_the_Alps" class="mw-redirect" title="Valleys of the Alps">valleys of the Alps</a> in the 8th century, forming Alemannia. Modern-day Switzerland was therefore then divided between the kingdoms of Alemannia and <a href="/wiki/Burgundy_(region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Burgundy (region)">Burgundy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_41-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early-41">[35]</a></sup> The entire region became part of the expanding <a href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankish Empire">Frankish Empire</a> in the 6th century, following <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis I</a>'s victory over the Alemanni at <a href="/wiki/Tolbiac" class="mw-redirect" title="Tolbiac">Tolbiac</a> in 504 AD, and later Frankish domination of the Burgundians.<sup id="cite_ref-Nationsencyclopedia_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsencyclopedia-43">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nationsonline_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsonline-44">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Throughout the rest of the 6th, 7th and 8th centuries the Swiss regions continued under Frankish hegemony (<a href="/wiki/Merovingians" class="mw-redirect" title="Merovingians">Merovingian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian</a> dynasties). But after its extension under <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankish Empire">Frankish Empire</a> was divided by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Verdun" title="Treaty of Verdun">Treaty of Verdun</a> in 843.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_41-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early-41">[35]</a></sup> The territories of present-day Switzerland became divided into <a href="/wiki/Middle_Francia" title="Middle Francia">Middle Francia</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Francia" title="East Francia">East Francia</a> until they were reunified under the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> around 1000&nbsp;AD.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_41-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early-41">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By 1200, the Swiss plateau comprised the dominions of the houses of <a href="/wiki/House_of_Savoy" title="House of Savoy">Savoy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%A4hringer" class="mw-redirect" title="Zähringer">Zähringer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Habsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburg">Habsburg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/House_of_Kyburg" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Kyburg">Kyburg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_41-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early-41">[35]</a></sup> Some regions (<a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Uri" title="Canton of Uri">Uri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Schwyz" title="Canton of Schwyz">Schwyz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unterwalden" title="Unterwalden">Unterwalden</a>, later known as <i>Waldstätten</i>) were accorded the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_immediacy" title="Imperial immediacy">Imperial immediacy</a> to grant the empire direct control over the mountain passes. With the extinction of its male line in 1263, the Kyburg dynasty fell in AD 1264; then the Habsburgs under <a href="/wiki/Rudolph_I_of_Habsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Rudolph I of Habsburg">King Rudolph I</a> (Holy Roman Emperor in 1273) laid claim to the Kyburg lands and annexed them extending their territory to the eastern Swiss plateau.<sup id="cite_ref-Nationsencyclopedia_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsencyclopedia-43">[37]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Swiss_Confederacy">Old Swiss Confederacy</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Swiss_Confederacy" title="Old Swiss Confederacy">Old Swiss Confederacy</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_the_Old_Swiss_Confederacy" title="Growth of the Old Swiss Confederacy">Growth of the Old Swiss Confederacy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Switzerland" title="Reformation in Switzerland">Reformation in Switzerland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Switzerland" title="Early Modern Switzerland">Early Modern Switzerland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Old_Swiss_Confederation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Old_Swiss_Confederation.jpg/220px-Old_Swiss_Confederation.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Old_Swiss_Confederation.jpg/330px-Old_Swiss_Confederation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Old_Swiss_Confederation.jpg/440px-Old_Swiss_Confederation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="712"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Old_Swiss_Confederation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Old Swiss Confederacy from 1291 (dark green) to the sixteenth century (light green) and its associates (blue). In the other colours shown are the subject territories.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Old Swiss Confederacy was an alliance among the valley communities of the central Alps. The Confederacy, governed by <a href="/wiki/Swiss_nobility" title="Swiss nobility">nobles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patrician_(post-Roman_Europe)" title="Patrician (post-Roman Europe)">patricians</a> of various cantons, facilitated management of common interests and ensured peace on the important mountain trade routes. The <a href="/wiki/Federal_Charter_of_1291" title="Federal Charter of 1291">Federal Charter of 1291</a> agreed between the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_commune" title="Medieval commune">rural communes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Uri" title="Canton of Uri">Uri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Schwyz" title="Canton of Schwyz">Schwyz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Unterwalden" title="Unterwalden">Unterwalden</a> is considered the confederacy's founding document, even though similar alliances are likely to have existed decades earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-schwabe_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schwabe-45">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By 1353, the three original <a href="/wiki/Cantons_of_Switzerland" title="Cantons of Switzerland">cantons</a> had joined with the cantons of <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Glarus" title="Canton of Glarus">Glarus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Zug" title="Canton of Zug">Zug</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lucerne" title="Lucerne">Lucerne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bern" title="Bern">Bern</a> city states to form the "Old Confederacy" of eight states that existed until the end of the 15th century. The expansion led to increased power and wealth for the confederation.<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> By 1460, the confederates controlled most of the territory south and west of the Rhine to the Alps and the Jura mountains, particularly after victories against the Habsburgs (<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sempach" title="Battle of Sempach">Battle of Sempach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_N%C3%A4fels" title="Battle of Näfels">Battle of Näfels</a>), over <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Bold" title="Charles the Bold">Charles the Bold</a> of <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Burgundy" title="Duchy of Burgundy">Burgundy</a> during the 1470s, and the success of the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_mercenaries" title="Swiss mercenaries">Swiss mercenaries</a>. The Swiss victory in the <a href="/wiki/Swabian_War" title="Swabian War">Swabian War</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Swabian_League" title="Swabian League">Swabian League</a> of <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Maximilian I, Holy Roman Emperor">Maximilian I</a> in 1499 amounted to <i>de facto</i> independence within the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> In 1501, Basel and Schaffhausen joined the Old Swiss Confederacy. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bundesbrief.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Bundesbrief.jpg/220px-Bundesbrief.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Bundesbrief.jpg/330px-Bundesbrief.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Bundesbrief.jpg/440px-Bundesbrief.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1030" data-file-height="908"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bundesbrief.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The 1291 <i>Bundesbrief</i> (federal charter)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Old Swiss Confederacy had acquired a reputation of invincibility during these earlier wars, but <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_the_Old_Swiss_Confederacy" title="Growth of the Old Swiss Confederacy">expansion of the confederation</a> suffered a setback in 1515 with the Swiss defeat in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marignano" title="Battle of Marignano">Battle of Marignano</a>. This ended the so-called "heroic" epoch of Swiss history.<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> The success of <a href="/wiki/Zwingli" class="mw-redirect" title="Zwingli">Zwingli</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Switzerland" title="Reformation in Switzerland">Reformation</a> in some cantons led to inter-cantonal religious conflicts in 1529 and 1531 (<a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Kappel" title="Wars of Kappel">Wars of Kappel</a>). It was not until more than one hundred years after these internal wars that, in 1648, under the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a>, European countries recognised Switzerland's independence from the Holy Roman Empire and its <a href="/wiki/Neutral_country" title="Neutral country">neutrality</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nationsencyclopedia_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsencyclopedia-43">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nationsonline_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsonline-44">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Switzerland" title="Early Modern Switzerland">Early Modern</a> period of Swiss history, the growing <a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">authoritarianism</a> of the patriciate families combined with a financial crisis in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> led to the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_peasant_war_of_1653" title="Swiss peasant war of 1653">Swiss peasant war of 1653</a>. In the background to this struggle, the conflict between <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Switzerland" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism in Switzerland">Catholic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protestantism_in_Switzerland" title="Protestantism in Switzerland">Protestant</a> cantons persisted, erupting in further violence at the <a href="/wiki/First_War_of_Villmergen" title="First War of Villmergen">First War of Villmergen</a>, in 1656, and the <a href="/wiki/Toggenburg_War" title="Toggenburg War">Toggenburg War</a> (or Second War of Villmergen), in 1712.<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Napoleonic_era">Napoleonic era</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Switzerland_in_the_Napoleonic_era" title="Switzerland in the Napoleonic era">Switzerland in the Napoleonic era</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helvetic_Republic" title="Helvetic Republic">Helvetic Republic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Mediation" title="Act of Mediation">Act of Mediation</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Acte_de_M%C3%A9diation_mg_0643.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Acte_de_M%C3%A9diation_mg_0643.jpg/220px-Acte_de_M%C3%A9diation_mg_0643.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Acte_de_M%C3%A9diation_mg_0643.jpg/330px-Acte_de_M%C3%A9diation_mg_0643.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Acte_de_M%C3%A9diation_mg_0643.jpg/440px-Acte_de_M%C3%A9diation_mg_0643.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3467" data-file-height="2681"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Acte_de_M%C3%A9diation_mg_0643.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Act of Mediation was Napoleon's attempt at a compromise between the <a href="/wiki/Ancien_R%C3%A9gime" title="Ancien Régime">Ancien Régime</a> and a Republic.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1798, the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">revolutionary French</a> government invaded Switzerland and imposed a new unified constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> This centralised the government of the country, effectively abolishing the cantons: moreover, <a href="/wiki/Mulhouse" title="Mulhouse">Mülhausen</a> joined France and the <a href="/wiki/Valtellina" title="Valtellina">Valtellina</a> valley became part of the <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Republic" title="Cisalpine Republic">Cisalpine Republic</a>, separating from Switzerland. The new <a href="/wiki/Regime" title="Regime">regime</a>, known as the Helvetic Republic, was highly unpopular. It had been imposed by a foreign invading army and destroyed centuries of tradition, making Switzerland nothing more than a French <a href="/wiki/Satellite_state" title="Satellite state">satellite state</a>. The fierce French suppression of the <a href="/wiki/Nidwalden" title="Nidwalden">Nidwalden Revolt</a> in September 1798 was an example of the oppressive presence of the <a href="/wiki/French_Army" title="French Army">French Army</a> and the local population's resistance to the occupation. </p>
      <p>When war broke out between France and its rivals, Russian and <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Monarchy" title="Habsburg Monarchy">Austrian</a> forces invaded Switzerland. The Swiss refused to fight alongside the French in the name of the Helvetic Republic. In 1803 <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a> organised a meeting of the leading Swiss politicians from both sides in Paris. The result was the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Mediation" title="Act of Mediation">Act of Mediation</a> which largely restored Swiss autonomy and introduced a Confederation of 19 cantons.<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> Henceforth, much of Swiss politics would concern balancing the cantons' tradition of self-rule with the need for a central government. </p>
      <p>In 1815 the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> fully re-established Swiss independence and the European powers agreed to permanently recognise Swiss neutrality.<sup id="cite_ref-Nationsencyclopedia_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsencyclopedia-43">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nationsonline_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsonline-44">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> Swiss troops still served foreign governments until 1860 when they fought in the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Gaeta_(1860)" title="Siege of Gaeta (1860)">Siege of Gaeta</a>. The treaty also allowed Switzerland to increase its territory, with the admission of the cantons of <a href="/wiki/Valais" title="Valais">Valais</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Neuch%C3%A2tel" title="Canton of Neuchâtel">Neuchâtel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Geneva" title="Canton of Geneva">Geneva</a>. Switzerland's borders have not changed since, except for some minor adjustments.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[41]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Federal_state">Federal state</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Restoration_and_Regeneration_(Switzerland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Restoration and Regeneration (Switzerland)">Restoration and Regeneration (Switzerland)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland_as_a_federal_state" title="Switzerland as a federal state">Switzerland as a federal state</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bern,_Federal_Palace,_1857.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Bern%2C_Federal_Palace%2C_1857.jpg/220px-Bern%2C_Federal_Palace%2C_1857.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Bern%2C_Federal_Palace%2C_1857.jpg/330px-Bern%2C_Federal_Palace%2C_1857.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Bern%2C_Federal_Palace%2C_1857.jpg/440px-Bern%2C_Federal_Palace%2C_1857.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2141" data-file-height="1349"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bern,_Federal_Palace,_1857.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The first <a href="/wiki/Federal_Palace" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Palace">Federal Palace</a> in Bern (1857). One of the three cantons presiding over the <a href="/wiki/Tagsatzung" title="Tagsatzung">Tagsatzung</a> (former legislative and executive council), Bern was chosen as the permanent seat of federal legislative and executive institutions in 1848, in part because of its closeness to the French-speaking area.<sup id="cite_ref-Bundesstadt_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bundesstadt-2">[1]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The restoration of power to the patriciate was only temporary. After a period of unrest with repeated violent clashes, such as the <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCriputsch" title="Züriputsch">Züriputsch</a> of 1839, civil war (the <i><a href="/wiki/Sonderbundskrieg" class="mw-redirect" title="Sonderbundskrieg">Sonderbundskrieg</a></i>) broke out in 1847 when some Catholic cantons tried to set up a separate alliance (the <i>Sonderbund</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> The war lasted for less than a month, causing fewer than 100 casualties, most of which were through <a href="/wiki/Friendly_fire" title="Friendly fire">friendly fire</a>. Yet however minor the Sonderbundskrieg appears compared with other European riots and wars in the 19th century, it nevertheless had a major impact on both the psychology and the society of the Swiss and of Switzerland. </p>
      <p>The war convinced most Swiss of the need for unity and strength towards its European neighbours. Swiss people from all strata of society, whether Catholic or Protestant, from the liberal or conservative current, realised that the cantons would profit more if their economic and religious interests were merged. </p>
      <p>Thus, while the rest of Europe saw <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">revolutionary uprisings</a>, the Swiss drew up a constitution which provided for a <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Federal_Constitution" title="Swiss Federal Constitution">federal layout</a>, much of it inspired by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Constitution">American example</a>. This constitution provided for a central authority while leaving the cantons the right to self-government on local issues. Giving credit to those who favoured the power of the cantons (the Sonderbund Kantone), the national assembly was divided between an <a href="/wiki/Upper_house" title="Upper house">upper house</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Council_of_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Swiss Council of States">Council of States</a>, two representatives per canton) and a <a href="/wiki/Lower_house" title="Lower house">lower house</a> (the <a href="/wiki/National_Council_of_Switzerland" class="mw-redirect" title="National Council of Switzerland">National Council</a>, with representatives elected from across the country). <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">Referendums</a> were made mandatory for any amendment of this constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-Nationsonline_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsonline-44">[38]</a></sup> This new constitution also brought a legal end to <a href="/wiki/Swiss_nobility#Current_situation" title="Swiss nobility">nobility in Switzerland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[42]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gotthard_Er%C3%B6ffnungszug_Bellinzona.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Gotthard_Er%C3%B6ffnungszug_Bellinzona.jpg/220px-Gotthard_Er%C3%B6ffnungszug_Bellinzona.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Gotthard_Er%C3%B6ffnungszug_Bellinzona.jpg/330px-Gotthard_Er%C3%B6ffnungszug_Bellinzona.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Gotthard_Er%C3%B6ffnungszug_Bellinzona.jpg/440px-Gotthard_Er%C3%B6ffnungszug_Bellinzona.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1984" data-file-height="1326"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gotthard_Er%C3%B6ffnungszug_Bellinzona.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Inauguration in 1882 of the <a href="/wiki/Gotthard_Rail_Tunnel" class="mw-redirect" title="Gotthard Rail Tunnel">Gotthard Rail Tunnel</a> connecting the southern canton of Ticino, the longest in the world at the time
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>A system of single weights and measures was introduced and in 1850 the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_franc" title="Swiss franc">Swiss franc</a> became the Swiss <a href="/wiki/Single_currency" class="mw-redirect" title="Single currency">single currency</a>. Article 11 of the constitution forbade sending troops to serve abroad, marking the end of foreign service. It came with the exception of serving the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a>, and the Swiss were still obliged to serve <a href="/wiki/Francis_II_of_the_Two_Sicilies" title="Francis II of the Two Sicilies">Francis II of the Two Sicilies</a> with Swiss Guards present at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Gaeta_(1860)" title="Siege of Gaeta (1860)">Siege of Gaeta in 1860</a>. </p>
      <p>An important clause of the constitution was that it could be re-written completely if this was deemed necessary, thus enabling it to evolve as a whole rather than being modified one amendment at a time.<sup id="cite_ref-HistoiredelaSuisse_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HistoiredelaSuisse-49">[43]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>This need soon proved itself when the rise in population and the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> that followed led to calls to modify the constitution accordingly. An early draft was rejected by the population in 1872 but modifications led to its acceptance in 1874.<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> It introduced the <a href="/wiki/Referendum#Switzerland" title="Referendum">facultative referendum</a> for laws at the federal level. It also established federal responsibility for defence, trade, and legal matters. </p>
      <p>In 1891, the constitution was revised with unusually strong elements of <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a>, which remain unique even today.<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Switzerland_during_the_World_Wars" title="Switzerland during the World Wars">Switzerland during the World Wars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_history_of_Switzerland" title="Modern history of Switzerland">Modern history of Switzerland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ulrich_Wille.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Ulrich_Wille.jpg/220px-Ulrich_Wille.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Ulrich_Wille.jpg/330px-Ulrich_Wille.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Ulrich_Wille.jpg/440px-Ulrich_Wille.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="448"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ulrich_Wille.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>General <a href="/wiki/Ulrich_Wille" title="Ulrich Wille">Ulrich Wille</a>, appointed commander-in-chief of the Swiss Army for the duration of World War I
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Switzerland was not invaded during either of the world wars. During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, Switzerland was home to the revolutionary and founder of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> Vladimir Illych Ulyanov (<a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a>) and he remained there until 1917.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[44]</a></sup> Swiss neutrality was seriously questioned by the <a href="/wiki/Grimm%E2%80%93Hoffmann_affair" title="Grimm–Hoffmann affair">Grimm–Hoffmann affair</a> in 1917, but that was short-lived. In 1920, Switzerland joined the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>, which was based in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, on condition that it was exempt from any military requirements. </p>
      <p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Tannenbaum" title="Operation Tannenbaum">detailed invasion plans</a> were drawn up by the Germans,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[45]</a></sup> but Switzerland was never attacked.<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> Switzerland was able to remain independent through a combination of military deterrence, concessions to Germany, and good fortune as larger events during the war delayed an invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-Nationsonline_44-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsonline-44">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stonebooks_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stonebooks-52">[46]</a></sup> Under General <a href="/wiki/Henri_Guisan" title="Henri Guisan">Henri Guisan</a>, appointed the <a href="/wiki/General_(Switzerland)" title="General (Switzerland)">commander-in-chief for the duration</a> of the war, a general mobilisation of the armed forces was ordered. The Swiss military strategy was changed from one of static defence at the borders to protect the economic heartland, to one of organised long-term attrition and withdrawal to strong, well-stockpiled positions high in the Alps known as the <a href="/wiki/National_Redoubt_(Switzerland)" title="National Redoubt (Switzerland)">Reduit</a>. Switzerland was an important base for espionage by both sides in the conflict and often mediated communications between the <a href="/wiki/Axis_Powers" class="mw-redirect" title="Axis Powers">Axis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> powers.<sup id="cite_ref-Stonebooks_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stonebooks-52">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Switzerland's trade was blockaded by both the Allies and by the Axis. Economic cooperation and extension of credit to the <a href="/wiki/Third_Reich" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Reich">Third Reich</a> varied according to the perceived likelihood of invasion and the availability of other trading partners. Concessions reached a peak after a crucial rail link through <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy France</a> was severed in 1942, leaving Switzerland (together with <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>) entirely isolated from the wider world by Axis controlled territory. Over the course of the war, Switzerland interned over 300,000 refugees<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-53">[47]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Red_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="International Red Cross">International Red Cross</a>, based in Geneva, played an important part during the conflict. Strict immigration and <a href="/wiki/Right_of_asylum" title="Right of asylum">asylum</a> policies as well as the financial relationships with <a href="/wiki/Nazi" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi">Nazi</a> Germany raised controversy, but not until the end of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[48]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the war, the Swiss Air Force engaged aircraft of both sides, shooting down 11 intruding <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a> planes in May and June 1940, then forcing down other intruders after a change of policy following threats from Germany. Over 100 Allied bombers and their crews were interned during the war. Between 1940 and 1945, <a href="/wiki/Bombings_of_Switzerland_in_World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombings of Switzerland in World War II">Switzerland was bombed by the Allies</a> causing fatalities and property damage.<sup id="cite_ref-Stonebooks_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stonebooks-52">[46]</a></sup> Among the cities and towns bombed were <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brusio" title="Brusio">Brusio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiasso" title="Chiasso">Chiasso</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornol" title="Cornol">Cornol</a>, Geneva, <a href="/wiki/Koblenz,_Switzerland" title="Koblenz, Switzerland">Koblenz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niederweningen" title="Niederweningen">Niederweningen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rafz" title="Rafz">Rafz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renens" title="Renens">Renens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samedan" title="Samedan">Samedan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schaffhausen" title="Schaffhausen">Schaffhausen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stein_am_Rhein" title="Stein am Rhein">Stein am Rhein</a>, <a href="/wiki/T%C3%A4gerwilen" title="Tägerwilen">Tägerwilen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thayngen" title="Thayngen">Thayngen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vals,_Switzerland" title="Vals, Switzerland">Vals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a>. Allied forces explained the bombings, which violated the 96th <a href="/wiki/Article_of_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Article of War">Article of War</a>, resulted from navigation errors, equipment failure, weather conditions, and errors made by bomber pilots. The Swiss expressed fear and concern that the bombings were intended to put pressure on Switzerland to end economic cooperation and neutrality with Nazi Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-helmreich_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helmreich-55">[49]</a></sup> Court-martial proceedings took place in England and the U.S. Government paid 62,176,433.06 in Swiss francs for reparations of the bombings. </p>
      <p>Switzerland's attitude towards <a href="/wiki/Switzerland_during_the_World_Wars#Refugees" title="Switzerland during the World Wars">refugees</a> was complicated and controversial; over the course of the war it admitted as many as 300,000 refugees<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-53">[47]</a></sup> while refusing tens of thousands more,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[50]</a></sup> including Jews who were severely persecuted by the Nazis. </p>
      <p>After the war, the Swiss government exported credits through the charitable fund known as the Schweizerspende and also donated to the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> to help Europe's recovery, efforts that ultimately benefited the Swiss economy.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[51]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, Swiss authorities <a href="/wiki/Switzerland_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="Switzerland and weapons of mass destruction">considered the construction</a> of a Swiss <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear bomb">nuclear bomb</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[52]</a></sup> Leading nuclear physicists at the <a href="/wiki/ETH_Z%C3%BCrich" class="mw-redirect" title="ETH Zürich">Federal Institute of Technology Zürich</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Paul_Scherrer" title="Paul Scherrer">Paul Scherrer</a> made this a realistic possibility. In 1988, the <a href="/wiki/Paul_Scherrer_Institute" title="Paul Scherrer Institute">Paul Scherrer Institute</a> was founded in his name to explore the therapeutic uses of <a href="/wiki/Neutron_scattering" title="Neutron scattering">neutron scattering</a> technologies. Financial problems with the defence budget and ethical considerations prevented the substantial funds from being allocated, and the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Non-Proliferation_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty">Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty</a> of 1968 was seen as a valid alternative. All remaining plans for building nuclear weapons were dropped by 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[53]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bundesrat_der_Schweiz_2003.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Bundesrat_der_Schweiz_2003.jpg/220px-Bundesrat_der_Schweiz_2003.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="305" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Bundesrat_der_Schweiz_2003.jpg/330px-Bundesrat_der_Schweiz_2003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Bundesrat_der_Schweiz_2003.jpg/440px-Bundesrat_der_Schweiz_2003.jpg 2x" data-file-width="938" data-file-height="1300"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bundesrat_der_Schweiz_2003.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>In 2003, by granting the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_People%27s_Party" title="Swiss People's Party">Swiss People's Party</a> a second seat in the governing cabinet, the Parliament altered the <a href="/wiki/Magic_formula" title="Magic formula">coalition</a> that had dominated Swiss politics since 1959.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Switzerland was the last Western republic to grant women the <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">right to vote</a>. Some Swiss cantons approved this in 1959, while at the federal level it was achieved in 1971<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FCO_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCO-60">[54]</a></sup> and, after resistance, in the last canton <a href="/wiki/Appenzell_Innerrhoden" title="Appenzell Innerrhoden">Appenzell Innerrhoden</a> (one of only two remaining <i><a href="/wiki/Landsgemeinde" title="Landsgemeinde">Landsgemeinde</a></i>, along with <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Glarus" title="Canton of Glarus">Glarus</a>) in 1990. After obtaining suffrage at the federal level, women quickly rose in political significance, with the first woman on the seven-member <a href="/wiki/Federal_Council_(Switzerland)" title="Federal Council (Switzerland)">Federal Council</a> executive being <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_Kopp" title="Elisabeth Kopp">Elisabeth Kopp</a>, who served from 1984 to 1989,<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> and the first female president being <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Dreifuss" title="Ruth Dreifuss">Ruth Dreifuss</a> in 1999. </p>
      <p>Switzerland joined the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> in 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-Nationsonline_44-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsonline-44">[38]</a></sup> In 1979 areas from the canton of <a href="/wiki/Bern" title="Bern">Bern</a> attained independence from the Bernese, forming the new <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Jura" title="Canton of Jura">canton of Jura</a>. On 18 April 1999 the Swiss population and the cantons voted in favour of a completely revised <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Federal_Constitution" title="Swiss Federal Constitution">federal constitution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2002 Switzerland became a full member of the United Nations, leaving the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Vatican City</a> as the last widely recognised state without full UN membership. Switzerland is a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">EFTA</a> but is not a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a>. An application for membership in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> was sent in May 1992, but not advanced since the EEA was rejected in December 1992<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_46-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief-46">[40]</a></sup> when Switzerland was the only country to launch a referendum on the EEA. There have since been several referendums on the EU issue; due to opposition from the citizens, the membership application has been withdrawn. Nonetheless, Swiss law is gradually being adjusted to conform with that of the EU, and the government has signed a number of <a href="/wiki/Bilateralism" title="Bilateralism">bilateral agreements</a> with the European Union. Switzerland, together with Liechtenstein, has been completely surrounded by the EU since Austria's entry in 1995. On 5 June 2005, Swiss voters agreed by a 55% majority to join the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Schengen treaty">Schengen treaty</a>, a result that was regarded by EU commentators as a sign of support by Switzerland, a country that is traditionally perceived as independent and reluctant to enter supranational bodies.<sup id="cite_ref-Nationsonline_44-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationsonline-44">[38]</a></sup> In September 2020, a referendum calling for a vote on end to the pact that allowed a free movement of people from the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> was introduced by the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_People%27s_Party" title="Swiss People's Party">Swiss People's Party</a> (SPP).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[55]</a></sup> However, the voters rejected the attempts of taking back control of <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a>, defeating the motion by a roughly 63%–37% margin.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[56]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Switzerland" title="Geography of Switzerland">Geography of Switzerland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Reliefkarte_Schweiz.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Reliefkarte_Schweiz.png/220px-Reliefkarte_Schweiz.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Reliefkarte_Schweiz.png/330px-Reliefkarte_Schweiz.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Reliefkarte_Schweiz.png/440px-Reliefkarte_Schweiz.png 2x" data-file-width="10129" data-file-height="6934"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Reliefkarte_Schweiz.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Physical map of Switzerland (in German)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Extending across the north and south side of the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">west</a>-<a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a>, Switzerland encompasses a great diversity of landscapes and climates on a limited area of 41,285 square kilometres (15,940&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Geo_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geo-63">[57]</a></sup> The population is about 8&nbsp;million, resulting in an average <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">population density</a> of around 195 people per square kilometre (500/sq&nbsp;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Geo_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geo-63">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-maps_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maps-64">[58]</a></sup> The more mountainous southern half of the country is far more sparsely populated than the northern half.<sup id="cite_ref-Geo_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geo-63">[57]</a></sup> In the largest Canton of <a href="/wiki/Graub%C3%BCnden" class="mw-redirect" title="Graubünden">Graubünden</a>, lying entirely in the Alps, population density falls to 27 /km<sup>2</sup> (70 /sq mi).<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[59]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Switzerland lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/45th_parallel_north" title="45th parallel north">45°</a> and <a href="/wiki/48th_parallel_north" title="48th parallel north">48° N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/5th_meridian_east" title="5th meridian east">5°</a> and <a href="/wiki/11th_meridian_east" title="11th meridian east">11° E</a>. It contains three basic topographical areas: the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Alps" title="Swiss Alps">Swiss Alps</a> to the south, the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Plateau" title="Swiss Plateau">Swiss Plateau</a> or Central Plateau, and the <a href="/wiki/Jura_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Jura mountains">Jura mountains</a> on the west. The Alps are a high mountain range running across the central-south of the country, constituting about 60% of the country's total area. The majority of the Swiss population live in the Swiss Plateau. Among the high valleys of the Swiss Alps, many glaciers are found, totalling an area of 1,063 square kilometres (410&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi). From these originate the headwaters of several major rivers, such as the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inn_(river)" title="Inn (river)">Inn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ticino_(river)" title="Ticino (river)">Ticino</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne" title="Rhône">Rhône</a>, which flow in the four cardinal directions into the whole of Europe. The hydrographic network includes several of the largest bodies of freshwater in Central and Western Europe, among which are included <a href="/wiki/Lake_Geneva" title="Lake Geneva">Lake Geneva</a> (also called le Lac Léman in French), <a href="/wiki/Lake_Constance" title="Lake Constance">Lake Constance</a> (known as Bodensee in German) and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Maggiore" title="Lake Maggiore">Lake Maggiore</a>. Switzerland has more than 1500 lakes and contains 6% of Europe's stock of fresh water. Lakes and glaciers cover about 6% of the national territory. The largest lake is Lake Geneva, in western Switzerland shared with France. The Rhône is both the main source and outflow of Lake Geneva. Lake Constance is the second-largest Swiss lake and, like Lake Geneva, an intermediate step by the Rhine at the border to Austria and Germany. While the Rhône flows into the Mediterranean Sea at the French Camargue region and the Rhine flows into the North Sea at Rotterdam in the Netherlands, about 1,000 kilometres (620 miles) apart, both springs are only about 22 kilometres (14 miles) apart from each other in the Swiss Alps.<sup id="cite_ref-Geo_63-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geo-63">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[60]</a></sup> </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Haute_route,_panorama_version.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Haute_route%2C_panorama_version.jpg/300px-Haute_route%2C_panorama_version.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="100" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Haute_route%2C_panorama_version.jpg/450px-Haute_route%2C_panorama_version.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Haute_route%2C_panorama_version.jpg/600px-Haute_route%2C_panorama_version.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="340"></a>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:View_from_Pilatus,_retouched.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/View_from_Pilatus%2C_retouched.jpg/300px-View_from_Pilatus%2C_retouched.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="100" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/View_from_Pilatus%2C_retouched.jpg/450px-View_from_Pilatus%2C_retouched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/View_from_Pilatus%2C_retouched.jpg/600px-View_from_Pilatus%2C_retouched.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8094" data-file-height="2686"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           Contrasted landscapes between the regions of the <a href="/wiki/Matterhorn" title="Matterhorn">Matterhorn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Lucerne" title="Lake Lucerne">Lake Lucerne</a>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Forty-eight of Switzerland's mountains are 4,000 metres (13,000&nbsp;ft) above sea in altitude or higher.<sup id="cite_ref-Geo_63-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geo-63">[57]</a></sup> At 4,634&nbsp;m (15,203&nbsp;ft), <a href="/wiki/Monte_Rosa" title="Monte Rosa">Monte Rosa</a> is the highest, although the <a href="/wiki/Matterhorn" title="Matterhorn">Matterhorn</a> (4,478&nbsp;m or 14,692&nbsp;ft) is often regarded as the most famous. Both are located within the <a href="/wiki/Pennine_Alps" title="Pennine Alps">Pennine Alps</a> in the canton of <a href="/wiki/Valais" title="Valais">Valais</a>, on the border with <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. The section of the <a href="/wiki/Bernese_Alps" title="Bernese Alps">Bernese Alps</a> above the deep glacial <a href="/wiki/Lauterbrunnen" title="Lauterbrunnen">Lauterbrunnen</a> valley, containing 72 waterfalls, is well known for the <a href="/wiki/Jungfrau" title="Jungfrau">Jungfrau</a> (4,158&nbsp;m or 13,642&nbsp;ft) <a href="/wiki/Eiger" title="Eiger">Eiger</a> and <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6nch" title="Mönch">Mönch</a>, and the many picturesque valleys in the region. In the southeast the long <a href="/wiki/Engadin" title="Engadin">Engadin</a> Valley, encompassing the <a href="/wiki/St._Moritz" title="St. Moritz">St. Moritz</a> area in canton of <a href="/wiki/Graub%C3%BCnden" class="mw-redirect" title="Graubünden">Graubünden</a>, is also well known; the highest peak in the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Bernina_Alps" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernina Alps">Bernina Alps</a> is <a href="/wiki/Piz_Bernina" title="Piz Bernina">Piz Bernina</a> (4,049&nbsp;m or 13,284&nbsp;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Geo_63-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geo-63">[57]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The more populous northern part of the country, constituting about 30% of the country's total area, is called the Swiss Plateau. It has greater open and hilly landscapes, partly forested, partly open pastures, usually with grazing herds, or vegetables and fruit fields, but it is still hilly. There are large lakes found here and the biggest Swiss cities are in this area of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Geo_63-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geo-63">[57]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Within Switzerland there are two small <a href="/wiki/Enclave" class="mw-redirect" title="Enclave">enclaves</a>: <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BCsingen" class="mw-redirect" title="Büsingen">Büsingen</a> belongs to Germany, <a href="/wiki/Campione_d%27Italia" title="Campione d'Italia">Campione d'Italia</a> belongs to Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[61]</a></sup> Switzerland has no exclaves in other countries. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_CHE_present.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Koppen-Geiger_Map_CHE_present.svg/220px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_CHE_present.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="103" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Koppen-Geiger_Map_CHE_present.svg/330px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_CHE_present.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Koppen-Geiger_Map_CHE_present.svg/440px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_CHE_present.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1816" data-file-height="850"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_CHE_present.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen-Geiger climate classification</a> map for Switzerland
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Swiss climate is generally <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">temperate</a>, but can vary greatly between the localities,<sup id="cite_ref-Climate_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Climate-68">[62]</a></sup> from glacial conditions on the mountaintops to the often pleasant near <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean climate</a> at Switzerland's southern tip. There are some valley areas in the southern part of Switzerland where some cold-hardy palm trees are found. Summers tend to be warm and humid at times with periodic rainfall so they are ideal for pastures and grazing. The less humid winters in the mountains may see long intervals of stable conditions for weeks, while the lower lands tend to suffer from <a href="/wiki/Inversion_(meteorology)" title="Inversion (meteorology)">inversion</a>, during these periods, thus seeing no sun for weeks. </p>
      <p>A weather phenomenon known as the <a href="/wiki/Foehn_wind" title="Foehn wind">föhn</a> (with an identical effect to the <a href="/wiki/Chinook_wind" title="Chinook wind">chinook wind</a>) can occur at all times of the year and is characterised by an unexpectedly warm wind, bringing air of very low relative humidity to the north of the Alps during rainfall periods on the southern face of the Alps. This works both ways across the alps but is more efficient if blowing from the south due to the steeper step for oncoming wind from the south. Valleys running south to north trigger the best effect. The driest conditions persist in all inner alpine valleys that receive less rain because arriving clouds lose a lot of their content while crossing the mountains before reaching these areas. Large alpine areas such as <a href="/wiki/Graub%C3%BCnden" class="mw-redirect" title="Graubünden">Graubünden</a> remain drier than pre-alpine areas and as in the main valley of the <a href="/wiki/Valais" title="Valais">Valais</a> wine grapes are grown there.<sup id="cite_ref-FederalClimateMaps_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FederalClimateMaps-69">[63]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The wettest conditions persist in the high Alps and in the <a href="/wiki/Ticino" title="Ticino">Ticino</a> canton which has much sun yet heavy bursts of rain from time to time.<sup id="cite_ref-FederalClimateMaps_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FederalClimateMaps-69">[63]</a></sup> Precipitation tends to be spread moderately throughout the year with a peak in summer. Autumn is the driest season, winter receives less precipitation than summer, yet the weather patterns in Switzerland are not in a stable climate system and can be variable from year to year with no strict and predictable periods. </p>
