          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:yellow; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;North America (33%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:blue; color:white;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Europe (24%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:brown; color:white;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Rest of the world (4%)
         </div> 
        </div> 
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Switzerland has a stable, prosperous and high-tech economy and enjoys great wealth, being ranked as the wealthiest country in the world per capita in multiple rankings. The country has been ranked as one of the <a href="/wiki/Corruption_Perceptions_Index" title="Corruption Perceptions Index">least corrupt countries in the world</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[112]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Banking_in_Switzerland#Links_to_illegal_activities" title="Banking in Switzerland">its banking sector</a> has been rated as "one of the most corrupt in the world".<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[113]</a></sup> It has the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">twentieth largest economy</a> by nominal <a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP)">thirty-eighth largest</a> by <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a>. It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_exports" title="List of countries by exports">seventeenth largest exporter</a>. Zürich and Geneva are regarded as <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">global cities</a>, ranked as <a href="/wiki/Globalization_and_World_Cities_Research_Network" title="Globalization and World Cities Research Network">Alpha and Beta</a> respectively. Basel is the capital of the pharmaceutical industry in Switzerland. With its world-class companies, Novartis and Roche, and many other players, it is also one of the world's most important centres for the life sciences industry.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[114]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Switzerland has the highest European rating in the <a href="/wiki/Index_of_Economic_Freedom" title="Index of Economic Freedom">Index of Economic Freedom</a> 2010, while also providing large coverage through public services.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[115]</a></sup> The nominal per capita <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a> is higher than those of the larger Western and Central European economies and Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-cia.gov_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-125">[116]</a></sup> In terms of <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">GDP per capita adjusted for purchasing power</a>, Switzerland was ranked 5th in the world in 2018 by World Bank<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[117]</a></sup> and estimated at 9th by the IMF in 2020,<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[118]</a></sup> as well as 11th by the CIA World Factbook in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[119]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The World Economic Forum's <a href="/wiki/Global_Competitiveness_Report" title="Global Competitiveness Report">Global Competitiveness Report</a> currently ranks Switzerland's economy as the most competitive in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[120]</a></sup> while ranked by the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> as Europe's most innovative country.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-urlEuropean_Innovation_Scoreboard_-_European_Commission_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-urlEuropean_Innovation_Scoreboard_-_European_Commission-131">[122]</a></sup> It is a relatively easy place to do business, currently ranking 20th of 189 countries in the <a href="/wiki/Ease_of_Doing_Business_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="Ease of Doing Business Index">Ease of Doing Business Index</a>. The slow growth Switzerland experienced in the 1990s and the early 2000s has brought greater support for economic reforms and harmonisation with the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-economicsurvey2007_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economicsurvey2007-132">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[124]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>For much of the 20th century, Switzerland was the wealthiest country in Europe by a considerable margin (by GDP – per capita).<sup id="cite_ref-westeuro_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-westeuro-134">[125]</a></sup> Switzerland also has one of the world's largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_current_account_balance_as_a_percentage_of_GDP" title="List of countries by current account balance as a percentage of GDP">account balances as a percentage of GDP</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[126]</a></sup> In 2018, the canton of Basel-City had the highest GDP per capita in the country, ahead of the cantons of Zug and Geneva.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[127]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Credit_Suisse" title="Credit Suisse">Credit Suisse</a>, only about 37% of residents own their own homes, one of the lowest rates of <a href="/wiki/Home_ownership" class="mw-redirect" title="Home ownership">home ownership</a> in Europe. Housing and food price levels were 171% and 145% of the <a href="/wiki/EU-25" class="mw-redirect" title="EU-25">EU-25</a> index in 2007, compared to 113% and 104% in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-yearbook2008_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yearbook2008-137">[128]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Switzerland is home to several large multinational corporations. The largest Swiss companies by revenue are <a href="/wiki/Glencore" title="Glencore">Glencore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gunvor_(company)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gunvor (company)">Gunvor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nestl%C3%A9" title="Nestlé">Nestlé</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Shipping_Company" title="Mediterranean Shipping Company">Mediterranean Shipping Company</a>, <a href="/wiki/Novartis" title="Novartis">Novartis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hoffmann-La_Roche" title="Hoffmann-La Roche">Hoffmann-La Roche</a>, <a href="/wiki/ABB_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="ABB Group">ABB</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercuria_Energy_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Mercuria Energy Group">Mercuria Energy Group</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adecco" class="mw-redirect" title="Adecco">Adecco</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[129]</a></sup> Also, notable are <a href="/wiki/UBS_AG" class="mw-redirect" title="UBS AG">UBS AG</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zurich_Financial_Services" class="mw-redirect" title="Zurich Financial Services">Zurich Financial Services</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richemont" title="Richemont">Richemont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Credit_Suisse" title="Credit Suisse">Credit Suisse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barry_Callebaut" title="Barry Callebaut">Barry Callebaut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Re" title="Swiss Re">Swiss Re</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rolex" title="Rolex">Rolex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tetra_Pak" title="Tetra Pak">Tetra Pak</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Swatch_Group" title="The Swatch Group">The Swatch Group</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swiss_International_Air_Lines" title="Swiss International Air Lines">Swiss International Air Lines</a>. Switzerland is ranked as having one of the most powerful economies in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-westeuro_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-westeuro-134">[125]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (November 2020)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Switzerland#Dubious" title="Talk:Switzerland">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Switzerland's most important economic sector is manufacturing. Manufacturing consists largely of the production of specialist <a href="/wiki/Chemical_industry" title="Chemical industry">chemicals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_industry_in_Switzerland" title="Pharmaceutical industry in Switzerland">health and pharmaceutical</a> goods, scientific and precision <a href="/wiki/Measuring_instrument" title="Measuring instrument">measuring instruments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Musical_instrument" title="Musical instrument">musical instruments</a>. The largest exported goods are chemicals (34% of exported goods), machines/electronics (20.9%), and precision instruments/watches (16.9%).<sup id="cite_ref-yearbook2008_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yearbook2008-137">[128]</a></sup> Exported services amount to a third of exports.<sup id="cite_ref-yearbook2008_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yearbook2008-137">[128]</a></sup> The service sector – especially <a href="/wiki/Banking" class="mw-redirect" title="Banking">banking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insurance" title="Insurance">insurance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">tourism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/International_organization" title="International organization">international organisations</a> – is another important industry for Switzerland. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Engadine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Engadine.jpg/220px-Engadine.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Engadine.jpg/330px-Engadine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Engadine.jpg/440px-Engadine.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1107"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Engadine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The high valley of <a href="/wiki/Engadin" title="Engadin">Engadine</a>. Tourism constitutes an important revenue for the less industrialised alpine regions.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>Agricultural protectionism—a rare exception to Switzerland's free trade policies—has contributed to high <a href="/wiki/Food_prices" title="Food prices">food prices</a>. Product market liberalisation is lagging behind many <a href="/wiki/Member_state_of_the_European_Union" title="Member state of the European Union">EU countries</a> according to the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-economicsurvey2007_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economicsurvey2007-132">[123]</a></sup> Nevertheless, domestic <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power" title="Purchasing power">purchasing power</a> is one of the best in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[132]</a></sup> Apart from agriculture, economic and trade barriers between the European Union and Switzerland are minimal and Switzerland has free trade agreements worldwide. Switzerland is a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">European Free Trade Association</a> (EFTA). </p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation_and_government_spending">Taxation and government spending</span></h3> 
      <p>Switzerland has an overwhelmingly private sector economy and low tax rates by <a href="/wiki/Western_World" class="mw-redirect" title="Western World">Western World</a> standards; <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_tax_revenue_as_percentage_of_GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries by tax revenue as percentage of GDP">overall taxation is one of the smallest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed countries</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Federal_budget_of_Switzerland" title="Federal budget of Switzerland">Swiss Federal budget</a> had a size of 62.8 billion Swiss francs in 2010, which is an equivalent 11.35% of the country's GDP in that year; however, the regional (canton) budgets and the budgets of the municipalities are not counted as part of the federal budget and the total rate of <a href="/wiki/Government_spending" title="Government spending">government spending</a> is closer to 33.8% of GDP. The main sources of income for the federal government are the <a href="/wiki/Value-added_tax" title="Value-added tax">value-added tax</a> (accounting for 33% of tax revenue) and the direct federal tax (29%), with the main areas of expenditure in social welfare and finance/taxes. The expenditures of the Swiss Confederation have been growing from 7% of GDP in 1960 to 9.7% in 1990 and to 10.7% in 2010. While the sectors social welfare and finance &amp; tax have been growing from 35% in 1990 to 48.2% in 2010, a significant reduction of expenditures has been occurring in the sectors of agriculture and national defence; from 26.5% in to 12.4% (estimation for the year 2015).<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[134]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour_market">Labour market</span></h3> 
      <p>Slightly more than 5 million people work in Switzerland;<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[135]</a></sup> about 25% of employees belonged to a trade union in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[136]</a></sup> Switzerland has a more flexible <a href="/wiki/Job_market" class="mw-redirect" title="Job market">job market</a> than neighbouring countries and the <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a> rate is very low. The unemployment rate increased from a low of 1.7% in June 2000 to a peak of 4.4% in December 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[137]</a></sup> The unemployment rate decreased to 3.2% in 2014 and held steady at that level for several years,<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[138]</a></sup> before further dropping to 2.5% in 2018 and 2.3% in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[139]</a></sup> Population growth from net immigration is quite high, at 0.52% of population in 2004, increased in the following years before falling to 0.54% again in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-yearbook2008_137-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yearbook2008-137">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[140]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_immigrant_population" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries by immigrant population">foreign citizen population</a> was 28.9% in 2015, about the same as in Australia. GDP per hour worked is the world's 16th highest, at 49.46 <a href="/wiki/International_dollar" title="International dollar">international dollars</a> in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[141]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2016, the median monthly gross salary in Switzerland was 6,502 francs per month (equivalent to US$6,597 per month), is just enough to cover the high cost of living. After rent, taxes and social security contributions, plus spending on goods and services, the average household has about 15% of its gross income left for savings. Though 61% of the population made less than the average income, income inequality is relatively low with a <a href="/wiki/Gini_coefficient" title="Gini coefficient">Gini coefficient</a> of 29.7, placing Switzerland among the top 20 countries for income equality. </p>
      <p>About 8.2% of the population live below the national poverty line, defined in Switzerland as earning less than CHF3,990 per month for a household of two adults and two children, and a further 15% are at risk of poverty. Single-parent families, those with no post-compulsory education and those who are out of work are among the most likely to be living below the poverty line. Although getting a job is considered a way out of poverty, among the gainfully employed, some 4.3% are considered working poor. One in ten jobs in Switzerland is considered low-paid and roughly 12% of Swiss workers hold such jobs, many of them women and foreigners. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_science">Education and science</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg/220px-11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg/330px-11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg/440px-11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="4288"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:11-11-24-basel-by-ralfr-035.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Basel" title="University of Basel">University of Basel</a> is Switzerland's oldest university (1460).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Switzerland" title="Education in Switzerland">Education in Switzerland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_Switzerland" title="Science and technology in Switzerland">Science and technology in Switzerland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Swiss_scientists.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Swiss_scientists.jpg/220px-Swiss_scientists.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Swiss_scientists.jpg/330px-Swiss_scientists.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Swiss_scientists.jpg/440px-Swiss_scientists.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Swiss_scientists.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Some Swiss scientists who played a key role in their discipline (clockwise):
         <br><a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> (mathematics)
         <br><a href="/wiki/Louis_Agassiz" title="Louis Agassiz">Louis Agassiz</a> (glaciology)
         <br><a href="/wiki/Auguste_Piccard" title="Auguste Piccard">Auguste Piccard</a> (aeronautics)
         <br><a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> (physics)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Education in Switzerland is very diverse because the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Switzerland" class="mw-redirect" title="Constitution of Switzerland">constitution of Switzerland</a> delegates the authority for the school system to the <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Switzerland" class="mw-redirect" title="Canton of Switzerland">cantons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Education_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Education-151">[142]</a></sup> There are both public and private schools, including many private international schools. The minimum age for primary school is about six years in all cantons, but most cantons provide a free "children's school" starting at four or five years old.<sup id="cite_ref-Education_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Education-151">[142]</a></sup> Primary school continues until grade four, five or six, depending on the school. Traditionally, the first foreign language in school was always one of the other national languages, although in 2000 English was introduced first in a few cantons.<sup id="cite_ref-Education_151-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Education-151">[142]</a></sup>At the end of primary school (or at the beginning of secondary school), pupils are separated according to their capacities in several (often three) sections. The fastest learners are taught advanced classes to be prepared for further studies and the <a href="/wiki/Matura" title="Matura">matura</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Education_151-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Education-151">[142]</a></sup> while students who assimilate a little more slowly receive an education more adapted to their needs. </p>
      <p>There are <a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_Switzerland" title="List of universities in Switzerland">12 universities in Switzerland</a>, ten of which are maintained at <a href="/wiki/Cantons_of_Switzerland" title="Cantons of Switzerland">cantonal</a> level and usually offer a range of non-technical subjects. The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Basel" title="University of Basel">first university in Switzerland</a> was founded in 1460 in <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a> (with a faculty of medicine) and has a tradition of chemical and medical research in Switzerland. It is listed 87th on the 2019 <a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">Academic Ranking of World Universities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[143]</a></sup> The largest university in Switzerland is the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Zurich" title="University of Zurich">University of Zurich</a> with nearly 25,000 students.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Federal_Institute_of_Technology_Zurich" class="mw-redirect" title="Swiss Federal Institute of Technology Zurich">Swiss Federal Institute of Technology Zurich</a> (ETHZ) and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Zurich" title="University of Zurich">University of Zurich</a> are listed 20th and 54th respectively, on the 2015 <a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">Academic Ranking of World Universities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[146]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The two institutes sponsored by the federal government are the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Federal_Institute_of_Technology_Zurich" class="mw-redirect" title="Swiss Federal Institute of Technology Zurich">Swiss Federal Institute of Technology Zurich</a> (ETHZ) in <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a>, founded 1855 and the <a href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_Polytechnique_F%C3%A9d%C3%A9rale_de_Lausanne" title="École Polytechnique Fédérale de Lausanne">EPFL</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lausanne" title="Lausanne">Lausanne</a>, founded 1969 as such, which was formerly an institute associated with the University of Lausanne.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[note 10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[147]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[148]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Eight of ten best <a href="/wiki/Hotel_school" class="mw-redirect" title="Hotel school">hotel schools</a> in the world are located in Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[149]</a></sup> In addition, there are various <a href="/wiki/Universities_of_Applied_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Universities of Applied Sciences">Universities of Applied Sciences</a>. In business and management studies, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_St._Gallen" title="University of St. Gallen">University of St. Gallen</a>, (HSG) is ranked 329th in the world according to <a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS World University Rankings</a><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[150]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Institute_for_Management_Development" title="International Institute for Management Development">International Institute for Management Development</a> (IMD), was ranked first in open programmes worldwide by the <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-rankings.ft.com_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rankings.ft.com-161">[151]</a></sup> Switzerland has the second highest rate (almost 18% in 2003) of foreign students in tertiary education, after Australia (slightly over 18%).<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[153]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As might befit a country that plays home to innumerable international organisations, the <a href="/wiki/Graduate_Institute_of_International_and_Development_Studies" title="Graduate Institute of International and Development Studies">Graduate Institute of International and Development Studies</a>, located in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, is not only continental Europe's oldest graduate school of international and development studies, but also widely believed to be one of its most prestigious.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[155]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Many <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> laureates have been Swiss scientists. They include the world-famous physicist <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[156]</a></sup> in the field of physics, who developed his <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a> while working in Bern. More recently <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Prelog" title="Vladimir Prelog">Vladimir Prelog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Rohrer" title="Heinrich Rohrer">Heinrich Rohrer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_R._Ernst" title="Richard R. Ernst">Richard Ernst</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmond_H._Fischer" title="Edmond H. Fischer">Edmond Fischer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rolf_Zinkernagel" class="mw-redirect" title="Rolf Zinkernagel">Rolf Zinkernagel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurt_W%C3%BCthrich" title="Kurt Wüthrich">Kurt Wüthrich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Dubochet" title="Jacques Dubochet">Jacques Dubochet</a> received Nobel Prizes in the sciences. In total, 114 Nobel Prize winners in all fields stand in relation to Switzerland<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[note 11]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> has been awarded nine times to organisations residing in Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-urlMueller_Science_-_Spezialitaeten:_Schweizer_Nobelpreisträger_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-urlMueller_Science_-_Spezialitaeten:_Schweizer_Nobelpreisträger-169">[158]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:LHC,_CERN.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/LHC%2C_CERN.jpg/220px-LHC%2C_CERN.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/LHC%2C_CERN.jpg/330px-LHC%2C_CERN.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/LHC%2C_CERN.jpg/440px-LHC%2C_CERN.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:LHC,_CERN.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">LHC</a> tunnel. CERN is the world's largest laboratory and also the birthplace of the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">World Wide Web</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[159]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Geneva and the nearby French department of <a href="/wiki/Ain" title="Ain">Ain</a> co-host the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Laboratory" title="Laboratory">laboratory</a>, <a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">CERN</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[160]</a></sup> dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a> research. Another important research centre is the <a href="/wiki/Paul_Scherrer_Institute" title="Paul Scherrer Institute">Paul Scherrer Institute</a>. Notable inventions include <a href="/wiki/Lysergic_acid_diethylamide" title="Lysergic acid diethylamide">lysergic acid diethylamide</a> (LSD), <a href="/wiki/Diazepam" title="Diazepam">diazepam</a> (Valium), the <a href="/wiki/Scanning_tunneling_microscope" title="Scanning tunneling microscope">scanning tunnelling microscope</a> (Nobel prize) and <a href="/wiki/Velcro" title="Velcro">Velcro</a>. Some technologies enabled the exploration of new worlds such as the pressurised balloon of <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Piccard" title="Auguste Piccard">Auguste Piccard</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bathyscaphe" title="Bathyscaphe">Bathyscaphe</a> which permitted <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Piccard" title="Jacques Piccard">Jacques Piccard</a> to reach the deepest point of the world's oceans. </p>
      <p>Switzerland Space Agency, the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Space_Office" title="Swiss Space Office">Swiss Space Office</a>, has been involved in various space technologies and programmes. In addition it was one of the 10 founders of <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">the European Space Agency</a> in 1975 and is the seventh largest contributor to the ESA budget. In the private sector, several companies are implicated in the space industry such as <a href="/wiki/Oerlikon_Space" class="mw-redirect" title="Oerlikon Space">Oerlikon Space</a><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[161]</a></sup> or Maxon Motors<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[162]</a></sup> who provide spacecraft structures. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Switzerland_and_the_European_Union">Switzerland and the European Union</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Switzerland%E2%80%93European_Union_relations" title="Switzerland–European Union relations">Switzerland–European Union relations</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Switzerland voted against membership in the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a> in a referendum in December 1992 and has since maintained and developed its relationships with the European Union (EU) and European countries through bilateral agreements. In March 2001, the Swiss people refused in a popular vote to start accession negotiations with the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[163]</a></sup> In recent years, the Swiss have brought their economic practices largely into conformity with those of the EU in many ways, in an effort to enhance their international competitiveness. The economy grew at 3% in 2010, 1.9% in 2011, and 1% in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-worldbank_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldbank-175">[164]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Future_enlargement_of_the_European_Union#States_not_on_the_agenda" class="mw-redirect" title="Future enlargement of the European Union">EU membership</a> was a long-term objective of the Swiss government, but there was and remains considerable popular sentiment against membership, which is opposed by the conservative <a href="/wiki/Swiss_People%27s_Party" title="Swiss People's Party">SVP</a> party, the largest party in the National Council, and not currently supported or proposed by several other political parties. The application for membership of the EU was formally withdrawn in 2016, having long been frozen. The western French-speaking areas and the urban regions of the rest of the country tend to be more pro-EU, nonetheless with far from a significant share of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[166]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:EU_and_EFTA.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/EU_and_EFTA.svg/220px-EU_and_EFTA.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/EU_and_EFTA.svg/330px-EU_and_EFTA.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/EU_and_EFTA.svg/440px-EU_and_EFTA.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="680" data-file-height="540"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:EU_and_EFTA.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Members of the <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">European Free Trade Association</a> (green) participate in the <a href="/wiki/European_Single_Market" title="European Single Market">European Single Market</a> and are part of the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Area" title="Schengen Area">Schengen Area</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The government has established an Integration Office under the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Department_of_Foreign_Affairs" title="Federal Department of Foreign Affairs">Department of Foreign Affairs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Department_of_Economic_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Department of Economic Affairs">Department of Economic Affairs</a>. To minimise the negative consequences of Switzerland's isolation from the rest of Europe, Bern and Brussels signed seven bilateral agreements to further liberalise trade ties. These agreements were signed in 1999 and took effect in 2001. This first series of bilateral agreements included the free movement of persons. A second series covering nine areas was signed in 2004 and has since been ratified, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Schengen Treaty">Schengen Treaty</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Dublin Convention">Dublin Convention</a> besides others.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[167]</a></sup> They continue to discuss further areas for cooperation.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[168]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2006, Switzerland approved 1 billion francs of supportive investment in the poorer Southern and Central European countries in support of cooperation and positive ties to the EU as a whole. A further referendum will be needed to approve 300 million francs to support Romania and Bulgaria and their recent admission. The Swiss have also been under EU and sometimes international pressure to reduce banking secrecy and to raise tax rates to parity with the EU. Preparatory discussions are being opened in four new areas: opening up the electricity market, participation in the European GNSS project <a href="/wiki/Galileo_positioning_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Galileo positioning system">Galileo</a>, cooperating with the European centre for disease prevention and recognising certificates of origin for food products.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[169]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 27 November 2008, the interior and justice ministers of the European Union in <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a> announced Switzerland's accession to the Schengen passport-free zone from 12 December 2008. The land <a href="/wiki/Border_checkpoint" title="Border checkpoint">border checkpoints</a> will remain in place only for goods movements, but should not run controls on people, though people entering the country had their <a href="/wiki/Passport" title="Passport">passports</a> checked until 29 March 2009 if they originated from a Schengen nation.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[170]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 9 February 2014, Swiss voters narrowly approved by 50.3% a ballot <a href="/wiki/Popular_initiative_(Switzerland)" title="Popular initiative (Switzerland)">initiative</a> launched by the <a href="/wiki/National_conservatism" title="National conservatism">national conservative</a> <a href="/wiki/Swiss_People%27s_Party" title="Swiss People's Party">Swiss People's Party (SVP/UDC)</a> to <a href="/wiki/Federal_popular_initiative_%22Against_mass_immigration%22" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal popular initiative &quot;Against mass immigration&quot;">restrict immigration</a>, and thus reintroducing a quota system on the influx of foreigners. This initiative was mostly backed by rural (57.6% approvals) and suburban agglomerations (51.2% approvals), and isolated towns (51.3% approvals) as well as by a strong majority (69.2% approval) in the canton of <a href="/wiki/Ticino" title="Ticino">Ticino</a>, while metropolitan centres (58.5% rejection) and the French-speaking part (58.5% rejection) rather rejected it.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[171]</a></sup> Some news commentators claim that this proposal <i>de facto</i> contradicts <a href="/wiki/Switzerland%E2%80%93European_Union_relations" title="Switzerland–European Union relations">the bilateral agreements</a> on the free movement of persons from these respective countries.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[173]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In December 2016, a political compromise with the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> was attained effectively canceling quotas on EU citizens but still allowing for favourable treatment of Swiss-based job applicants.<sup id="cite_ref-euobserver22216_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euobserver22216-185">[174]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 27 September 2020, Swiss voters clearly rejected the anti-free movement <a href="/wiki/Popular_initiative_(Switzerland)" title="Popular initiative (Switzerland)">popular initiative</a> by the conservative <a href="/wiki/Swiss_People%27s_Party" title="Swiss People's Party">Swiss People's Party</a> (SVP) with nearly 62% "no" votes, reflecting democratic support for bilateral agreements with the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[175]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Energy.2C_infrastructure_and_environment"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy,_infrastructure_and_environment">Energy, infrastructure and environment</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Switzerland" title="Energy in Switzerland">Energy in Switzerland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Switzerland" title="Transport in Switzerland">Transport in Switzerland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Waste_management_in_Switzerland" title="Waste management in Switzerland">Waste management in Switzerland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Environment_of_Switzerland" class="mw-redirect" title="Environment of Switzerland">Environment of Switzerland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mauvoisin_Dam.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Mauvoisin_Dam.jpg/280px-Mauvoisin_Dam.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Mauvoisin_Dam.jpg/420px-Mauvoisin_Dam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Mauvoisin_Dam.jpg/560px-Mauvoisin_Dam.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="461"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mauvoisin_Dam.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Switzerland has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_dams_in_the_world" class="mw-redirect" title="List of tallest dams in the world">tallest dams</a> in Europe, among which the <a href="/wiki/Mauvoisin_Dam" title="Mauvoisin Dam">Mauvoisin Dam</a>, in the Alps. Hydroelectricity is the most important domestic source of energy in the country.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">Electricity</a> generated in Switzerland is 56% from <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a> and 39% from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a>, resulting in a nearly CO<sub>2</sub>-free electricity-generating network. On 18 May 2003, two <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-nuclear">anti-nuclear</a> initiatives were turned down: <i>Moratorium Plus</i>, aimed at forbidding the building of new <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear power plants">nuclear power plants</a> (41.6% supported and 58.4% opposed),<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[176]</a></sup> and Electricity Without Nuclear (33.7% supported and 66.3% opposed) after a previous moratorium expired in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[177]</a></sup> However, as a reaction to the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima nuclear disaster</a>, the Swiss government announced in 2011 that it plans to end its use of nuclear energy in the next 2 or 3 decades.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[178]</a></sup> In November 2016, Swiss voters rejected a proposal by the <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_Switzerland" title="Green Party of Switzerland">Green Party</a> to accelerate the phaseout of nuclear power (45.8% supported and 54.2% opposed).<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[179]</a></sup> The Swiss Federal Office of Energy (SFOE) is the office responsible for all questions relating to energy supply and energy use within the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Department_of_Environment,_Transport,_Energy_and_Communications" title="Federal Department of Environment, Transport, Energy and Communications">Federal Department of Environment, Transport, Energy and Communications</a> (DETEC). The agency is supporting the <a href="/wiki/2000-watt_society" title="2000-watt society">2000-watt society</a> initiative to cut the nation's energy use by more than half by the year 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[180]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:L%C3%B6tschberg_Tunnel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/L%C3%B6tschberg_Tunnel.jpg/220px-L%C3%B6tschberg_Tunnel.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/L%C3%B6tschberg_Tunnel.jpg/330px-L%C3%B6tschberg_Tunnel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/L%C3%B6tschberg_Tunnel.jpg/440px-L%C3%B6tschberg_Tunnel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3488" data-file-height="2616"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:L%C3%B6tschberg_Tunnel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Entrance of the new <a href="/wiki/L%C3%B6tschberg_Base_Tunnel" title="Lötschberg Base Tunnel">Lötschberg Base Tunnel</a>, the third-longest railway tunnel in the world, under the old <a href="/wiki/L%C3%B6tschberg_railway_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Lötschberg railway line">Lötschberg railway line</a>. It was the first completed tunnel of the greater project <a href="/wiki/NRLA" title="NRLA">NRLA</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The most dense rail network in Europe<sup id="cite_ref-FCO_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCO-60">[54]</a></sup> of 5,250 kilometres (3,260&nbsp;mi) carries over 596 million passengers annually (as of 2015).<sup id="cite_ref-ÖVZR2015_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ÖVZR2015-192">[181]</a></sup> In 2015, each Swiss resident travelled on average 2,550 kilometres (1,580&nbsp;mi) by rail, which makes them the keenest rail users.<sup id="cite_ref-ÖVZR2015_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ÖVZR2015-192">[181]</a></sup> Virtually 100% of the network is electrified. The vast majority (60%) of the network is operated by the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Federal_Railways" title="Swiss Federal Railways">Swiss Federal Railways (SBB&nbsp;CFF&nbsp;FFS)</a>. Besides the second largest <a href="/wiki/Standard_gauge" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard gauge">standard gauge</a> railway company <a href="/wiki/BLS_AG" title="BLS AG">BLS AG</a> two railways companies operating on <a href="/wiki/Narrow_gauge" class="mw-redirect" title="Narrow gauge">narrow gauge</a> networks are the <a href="/wiki/Rhaetian_Railway" title="Rhaetian Railway">Rhaetian Railway (RhB)</a> in the southeastern canton of Graubünden, which includes some World Heritage lines,<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[182]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Matterhorn_Gotthard_Bahn" title="Matterhorn Gotthard Bahn">Matterhorn Gotthard Bahn (MGB)</a>, which co-operates together with RhB the <a href="/wiki/Glacier_Express" title="Glacier Express">Glacier Express</a> between <a href="/wiki/Zermatt" title="Zermatt">Zermatt</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Moritz" title="St. Moritz">St. Moritz</a>/<a href="/wiki/Davos" title="Davos">Davos</a>. On 31 May 2016 the <a href="/wiki/List_of_longest_tunnels" title="List of longest tunnels">world's longest and deepest railway tunnel</a> and the first flat, low-level route through the Alps, the 57.1-kilometre long (35.5&nbsp;mi) <a href="/wiki/Gotthard_Base_Tunnel" title="Gotthard Base Tunnel">Gotthard Base Tunnel</a>, opened as the largest part of the <a href="/wiki/NRLA" title="NRLA">New Railway Link through the Alps (NRLA)</a> project after 17 years of realization. It started its daily business for passenger transport on 11 December 2016 replacing the <a href="/wiki/Gotthard_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Gotthard line">old, mountainous, scenic route</a> over and through the <a href="/wiki/Gotthard_Pass" title="Gotthard Pass">St Gotthard Massif</a>. </p>
      <p>Switzerland has a publicly managed road network without <a href="/wiki/Road_toll_(modern)" class="mw-redirect" title="Road toll (modern)">road tolls</a> that is financed by highway permits as well as vehicle and gasoline taxes. The Swiss autobahn/autoroute system requires the purchase of a <a href="/wiki/Vignette_(road_tax)" title="Vignette (road tax)">vignette</a> (toll sticker)—which costs 40 <a href="/wiki/Swiss_franc" title="Swiss franc">Swiss francs</a>—for one calendar year in order to use its roadways, for both passenger cars and trucks. The Swiss autobahn/autoroute network has a total length of 1,638&nbsp;km (1,018&nbsp;mi) (as of 2000) and has, by an area of 41,290&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (15,940&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi), also one of the highest <a href="/wiki/Motorway" class="mw-redirect" title="Motorway">motorway</a> densities in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[183]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zurich_Airport" title="Zurich Airport">Zurich Airport</a> is Switzerland's largest international flight gateway, which handled 22.8 million passengers in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[184]</a></sup> The other international airports are <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Airport" title="Geneva Airport">Geneva Airport</a> (13.9 million passengers in 2012),<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[185]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/EuroAirport_Basel_Mulhouse_Freiburg" title="EuroAirport Basel Mulhouse Freiburg">EuroAirport Basel Mulhouse Freiburg</a> which is located in France, <a href="/wiki/Bern_Airport" title="Bern Airport">Bern Airport</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lugano_Airport" title="Lugano Airport">Lugano Airport</a>, <a href="/wiki/St._Gallen-Altenrhein_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Gallen-Altenrhein Airport">St. Gallen-Altenrhein Airport</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sion_Airport" title="Sion Airport">Sion Airport</a>. Swiss International Air Lines is the flag carrier of Switzerland. Its main hub is Zürich, but it is legally domiciled in Basel. </p>
      <p>Switzerland has one of the best environmental records among nations in the developed world;<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[186]</a></sup> it was one of the countries to sign the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a> in 1998 and ratified it in 2003. With <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Korea" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Korea">Republic of Korea</a> it forms the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Integrity_Group" title="Environmental Integrity Group">Environmental Integrity Group</a> (EIG).<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[187]</a></sup> The country is heavily active in recycling and anti-littering regulations and is one of the top recyclers in the world, with 66% to 96% of recyclable materials being recycled, depending on the area of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[188]</a></sup> The 2014 Global Green Economy Index ranked Switzerland among the top 10 green economies in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-ggei_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ggei-200">[189]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Switzerland developed an efficient system to recycle most recyclable materials.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[190]</a></sup> Publicly organised collection by volunteers and economical <a href="/wiki/Railway" class="mw-redirect" title="Railway">railway</a> transport logistics started as early as 1865 under the leadership of the notable industrialist Hans Caspar Escher (Escher Wyss AG) when the first modern Swiss <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a> manufacturing plant was built in <a href="/wiki/Biberist" title="Biberist">Biberist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[191]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Switzerland also has an economic system for garbage disposal, which is based mostly on recycling and energy-producing <a href="/wiki/Incinerator" class="mw-redirect" title="Incinerator">incinerators</a> due to a strong political will to protect the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[192]</a></sup> As in other European countries, the illegal disposal of garbage is not tolerated at all and heavily fined. In almost all Swiss municipalities, stickers or dedicated garbage bags need to be purchased that allow for the identification of disposable garbage.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[193]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Switzerland" title="Demographics of Switzerland">Demographics of Switzerland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swiss_people" title="Swiss people">Swiss people</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Swiss_people" title="List of Swiss people">List of Swiss people</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bev%C3%B6lkerungsdichte_der_Schweiz_2019.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Bev%C3%B6lkerungsdichte_der_Schweiz_2019.png/220px-Bev%C3%B6lkerungsdichte_der_Schweiz_2019.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Bev%C3%B6lkerungsdichte_der_Schweiz_2019.png/330px-Bev%C3%B6lkerungsdichte_der_Schweiz_2019.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Bev%C3%B6lkerungsdichte_der_Schweiz_2019.png/440px-Bev%C3%B6lkerungsdichte_der_Schweiz_2019.png 2x" data-file-width="4133" data-file-height="2848"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bev%C3%B6lkerungsdichte_der_Schweiz_2019.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Population density in Switzerland (2019)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ausl%C3%A4nderanteil_der_Schweiz_2019.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Ausl%C3%A4nderanteil_der_Schweiz_2019.png/220px-Ausl%C3%A4nderanteil_der_Schweiz_2019.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Ausl%C3%A4nderanteil_der_Schweiz_2019.png/330px-Ausl%C3%A4nderanteil_der_Schweiz_2019.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Ausl%C3%A4nderanteil_der_Schweiz_2019.png/440px-Ausl%C3%A4nderanteil_der_Schweiz_2019.png 2x" data-file-width="4133" data-file-height="2848"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ausl%C3%A4nderanteil_der_Schweiz_2019.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Percentage of foreigners in Switzerland (2019)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2018, Switzerland's population slightly exceeded 8.5 million. In common with other developed countries, the Swiss population increased rapidly during the industrial era, quadrupling between 1800 and 1990 and has continued to grow. Like most of Europe, Switzerland faces an <a href="/wiki/Aging_population" class="mw-redirect" title="Aging population">ageing population</a>, albeit with consistent annual growth projected into 2035, due mostly to immigration and a fertility rate close to <a href="/wiki/Replacement_fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Replacement fertility rate">replacement level</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[194]</a></sup> Switzerland subsequently has one of the oldest populations in the world, with the average age of 42.5 years.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[195]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Switzerland&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, resident foreigners make up 25.2% of the population, one of the largest proportions in the developed world.<sup id="cite_ref-population_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-population-12">[9]</a></sup> Most of these (64%) were from European Union or <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">EFTA</a> countries.<sup id="cite_ref-CHPopForeign2012_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHPopForeign2012-207">[196]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italians</a> were the largest single group of foreigners, with 15.6% of total foreign population, followed closely by Germans (15.2%), immigrants from Portugal (12.7%), France (5.6%), Serbia (5.3%), Turkey (3.8%), Spain (3.7%), and Austria (2%). Immigrants from <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, most of them former <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_Tamil" class="mw-redirect" title="Sri Lankan Tamil">Tamil</a> refugees, were the largest group among people of Asian origin (6.3%).<sup id="cite_ref-CHPopForeign2012_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHPopForeign2012-207">[196]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Additionally, the figures from 2012 show that 34.7% of the permanent resident population aged 15 or over in Switzerland (around 2.33 million), had an immigrant background. A third of this population (853,000) held Swiss citizenship. Four fifths of persons with an immigration background were themselves immigrants (first generation foreigners and native-born and naturalised Swiss citizens), whereas one fifth were born in Switzerland (second generation foreigners and native-born and naturalised Swiss citizens).<sup id="cite_ref-CHPopMig2012_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHPopMig2012-208">[197]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 2000s, domestic and international institutions expressed concern about what was perceived as an increase in <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia" title="Xenophobia">xenophobia</a>, particularly in some political campaigns. In reply to one critical report, the Federal Council noted that "racism unfortunately is present in Switzerland", but stated that the high proportion of foreign citizens in the country, as well as the generally unproblematic integration of foreigners, underlined Switzerland's openness.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[198]</a></sup> Follow-up study conducted in 2018 found that 59% considered racism a serious problem in Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[199]</a></sup> The proportion of the population that has reported being targeted by racial discrimination has increased in recent years, from 10% in 2014 to almost 17% in 2018, according to the Federal Statistical Office.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[200]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Drug use is comparable to other developed countries<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (November 2020)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Switzerland#Dubious" title="Talk:Switzerland">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> with 14% of men and 6.5% of women between 20 and 24 saying they had <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_in_Switzerland" title="Cannabis in Switzerland">consumed cannabis</a> in the past 30 days,<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[201]</a></sup> and 5 Swiss cities were listed among the top 10 European cities for <a href="/wiki/Legal_status_of_cocaine" title="Legal status of cocaine">cocaine use</a> as measured in wastewater.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[202]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[203]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Switzerland" title="Languages of Switzerland">Languages of Switzerland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Karte_Schweizer_Sprachgebiete_2017.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Karte_Schweizer_Sprachgebiete_2017.png/220px-Karte_Schweizer_Sprachgebiete_2017.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Karte_Schweizer_Sprachgebiete_2017.png/330px-Karte_Schweizer_Sprachgebiete_2017.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Karte_Schweizer_Sprachgebiete_2017.png/440px-Karte_Schweizer_Sprachgebiete_2017.png 2x" data-file-width="4133" data-file-height="2848"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Karte_Schweizer_Sprachgebiete_2017.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>National languages in Switzerland (2016): 
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#f7c5b4; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> (62.8%)
         </div> 
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#d9d4e9; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> (22.9%)
         </div> 
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#b6ddc7; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> (8.2%)
         </div> 
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#fffcc8; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Romansh_language" title="Romansh language">Romansh</a> (0.5%)
         </div><sup id="cite_ref-LangStat_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LangStat-215">[204]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Switzerland has four <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">national languages</a>: mainly <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> (spoken by 62.8% of the population in 2016); <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> (22.9%) in the west; and <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> (8.2%) in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-NL_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NL-216">[205]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LangStat_215-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LangStat-215">[204]</a></sup> The fourth national language, <a href="/wiki/Romansh_language" title="Romansh language">Romansh</a> (0.5%), is a <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance language</a> spoken locally in the southeastern trilingual <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Grisons" class="mw-redirect" title="Canton of Grisons">canton of Grisons</a>, and is designated by Article 4 of the Federal Constitution as a national language along with German, French, and Italian, and in Article 70 as an official language if the authorities communicate with persons who speak Romansh. However, federal laws and other official acts do not need to be decreed in Romansh. </p>
      <p>In 2016, the languages most spoken at home among permanent residents aged 15 and older were <a href="/wiki/Swiss_German" title="Swiss German">Swiss German</a> (59.4%), French (23.5%), <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">Standard German</a> (10.6%), and Italian (8.5%). Other languages spoken at home included English (5.0%), Portuguese (3.8%), Albanian (3.0%), Spanish (2.6%) and Serbian and Croatian (2.5%). 6.9% reported speaking another language at home.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[206]</a></sup> In 2014 almost two-thirds (64.4%) of the permanent resident population indicated speaking more than one language regularly.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[207]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The federal government is obliged to communicate in the official languages, and in the federal parliament simultaneous translation is provided from and into German, French and Italian.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[208]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Aside from the official forms of their respective languages, the four linguistic regions of Switzerland also have their local dialectal forms. The role played by dialects in each linguistic region varies dramatically: in the German-speaking regions, <a href="/wiki/Swiss_German" title="Swiss German">Swiss German</a> dialects have become ever more prevalent since the second half of the 20th century, especially in the media, such as radio and television, and are used as an everyday language for many, while the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Standard_German" title="Swiss Standard German">Swiss variety of Standard German</a> is almost always used instead of dialect for written communication (c.f. <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossic usage of a language</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-dialhls_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dialhls-220">[209]</a></sup> Conversely, in the French-speaking regions the local dialects have almost disappeared (only 6.3% of the population of Valais, 3.9% of Fribourg, and 3.1% of Jura still spoke dialects at the end of the 20th century), while in the Italian-speaking regions dialects are mostly limited to family settings and casual conversation.<sup id="cite_ref-dialhls_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dialhls-220">[209]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The principal official languages (German, French, and Italian) have terms, not used outside of Switzerland, known as <a href="/wiki/Helvetism" title="Helvetism">Helvetisms</a>. German Helvetisms are, roughly speaking, a large group of words typical of <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Standard_German" title="Swiss Standard German">Swiss Standard German</a>, which do not appear either in <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">Standard German</a>, nor in other German dialects. These include terms from Switzerland's surrounding language cultures (German <i>Billett</i><sup id="cite_ref-billete_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-billete-221">[210]</a></sup> from French), from similar terms in another language (Italian <i>azione</i> used not only as <i>act</i> but also as <i>discount</i> from German <i>Aktion</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-CHDuden_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHDuden-222">[211]</a></sup> The French spoken in Switzerland has similar terms, which are equally known as Helvetisms. The most frequent characteristics of Helvetisms are in vocabulary, phrases, and pronunciation, but certain Helvetisms denote themselves as special in syntax and orthography likewise. <a href="/wiki/Duden" title="Duden">Duden</a>, the comprehensive German dictionary, contains about 3000 Helvetisms.<sup id="cite_ref-CHDuden_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHDuden-222">[211]</a></sup> Current French dictionaries, such as the <a href="/wiki/Petit_Larousse" title="Petit Larousse">Petit Larousse</a>, include several hundred Helvetisms.<sup id="cite_ref-Clyne1992_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clyne1992-223">[212]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Learning one of the other national languages at school is compulsory for all Swiss pupils, so many Swiss are supposed to be at least <a href="/wiki/Multilingualism" title="Multilingualism">bilingual</a>, especially those belonging to linguistic minority groups.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[213]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Switzerland" title="Health in Switzerland">Health in Switzerland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Switzerland" title="Healthcare in Switzerland">Healthcare in Switzerland</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Swiss residents are universally required to buy health insurance from private insurance companies, which in turn are required to accept every applicant. While the cost of the system is among the highest, it compares well with other European countries in terms of health outcomes; patients have been reported as being, in general, highly satisfied with it.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[214]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[215]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[216]</a></sup> In 2012, life expectancy at birth was 80.4 years for men and 84.7 years for women<sup id="cite_ref-CHMortality_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHMortality-228">[217]</a></sup> — the highest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-WEFHCR_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WEFHCR-229">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CHLifeExpectancy_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHLifeExpectancy-230">[219]</a></sup> However, spending on health is particularly high at 11.4% of <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a> (2010), on par with Germany and France (11.6%) and other European countries, but notably less than spending in the USA (17.6%).<sup id="cite_ref-CHStatHealth2012_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHStatHealth2012-231">[220]</a></sup> From 1990, a steady increase can be observed, reflecting the high costs of the services provided.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-232">[221]</a></sup> With an ageing population and new healthcare technologies, health spending will likely continue to rise.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD_232-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-232">[221]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>It is estimated that one out of six persons in Switzerland suffers from <a href="/wiki/Mental_illness" class="mw-redirect" title="Mental illness">mental illness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[222]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urbanisation">Urbanisation</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cities_of_Switzerland" class="mw-redirect" title="Cities of Switzerland">Towns of Switzerland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Sion_de_la_Bisse_de_Clavau.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Sion_de_la_Bisse_de_Clavau.jpg/220px-Sion_de_la_Bisse_de_Clavau.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Sion_de_la_Bisse_de_Clavau.jpg/330px-Sion_de_la_Bisse_de_Clavau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Sion_de_la_Bisse_de_Clavau.jpg/440px-Sion_de_la_Bisse_de_Clavau.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Sion_de_la_Bisse_de_Clavau.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Urbanisation in the <a href="/wiki/Rhone" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhone">Rhone Valley</a> (outskirts of <a href="/wiki/Sion,_Switzerland" title="Sion, Switzerland">Sion</a>)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Between two thirds and three quarters of the population live in urban areas.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[223]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cities_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cities-235">[224]</a></sup> Switzerland has gone from a largely rural country to an urban one in just 70 years. Since 1935 urban development has claimed as much of the Swiss landscape as it did during the previous 2,000 years. This <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">urban sprawl</a> does not only affect the plateau but also the Jura and the Alpine foothills<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[225]</a></sup> and there are growing concerns about land use.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[226]</a></sup> However, from the beginning of the 21st century, the population growth in urban areas is higher than in the countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-Cities_235-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cities-235">[224]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Switzerland has a dense network of towns, where large, medium and small towns are complementary.<sup id="cite_ref-Cities_235-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cities-235">[224]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Swiss_plateau" class="mw-redirect" title="Swiss plateau">plateau</a> is very densely populated with about 450 people per km<sup>2</sup> and the landscape continually shows signs of human presence.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[227]</a></sup> The weight of the largest metropolitan areas, which are <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>–<a href="/wiki/Lausanne" title="Lausanne">Lausanne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bern" title="Bern">Bern</a> tend to increase.<sup id="cite_ref-Cities_235-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cities-235">[224]</a></sup> In international comparison the importance of these urban areas is stronger than their number of inhabitants suggests.<sup id="cite_ref-Cities_235-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cities-235">[224]</a></sup> In addition the three main centres of Zürich, Geneva and Basel are recognised for their particularly great quality of life.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[228]</a></sup> </p>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Switzerland" title="Religion in Switzerland">Religion in Switzerland</a>
      </div> 
      <table class="wikitable sortable plainrowheaders floatright" style="font-size: 90%; text-align:right;">

         <th scope="row" style="text-indent:15px;"><i>other religious communities</i> </th> 
         <td>0.3 </td> 
         <td align="left"><span style="display:none;">0.3</span>
          <div style="width:0.9px;height:2ex;background:#aaa;background:#87A96B">
           &nbsp;
          </div> </td>
